import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes, embed_dim=512, num_heads=8, num_layers=6, 
                 mlp_ratio=4.0, patch_size=4, in_channels=3, image_size=32):
        """
        Convolutional Vision Transformer (CViT) implementation.
        :param num_classes: Number of output classes for classification.
        :param embed_dim: Dimensionality of the embedding space.
        :param num_heads: Number of attention heads.
        :param num_layers: Number of transformer layers.
        :param mlp_ratio: Ratio of the MLP hidden dimension to the embedding dimension.
        :param patch_size: Size of the convolutional patches.
        :param in_channels: Number of input channels (e.g., 3 for RGB images).
        :param image_size: Height/width of the square input image.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()

        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.image_size = image_size
        self.embed_dim = embed_dim

        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, embed_dim, kernel_size=patch_size, stride=patch_size)
        num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2  # Total number of patches after conv
        self.linear_proj = nn.Linear(embed_dim * num_patches, embed_dim)

        self.transformer_layers = nn.ModuleList([
            nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(
                d_model=embed_dim,
                nhead=num_heads,
                dim_feedforward=int(embed_dim * mlp_ratio),
                dropout=0.0,
                batch_first=True
            ) for _ in range(num_layers)
        ])

        self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.zeros(1, 1, embed_dim))
        self.fc_out = nn.Linear(embed_dim, num_classes)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the CViT model.
        :param x: Input tensor of shape (B, C, H, W)
        :return: Output tensor of shape (B, num_classes)
        """
        B = x.size(0)
        x = self.conv1(x)                  # (B, embed_dim, H/patch_size, W/patch_size)
        x = x.flatten(start_dim=1)         # (B, embed_dim * num_patches)
        x = self.linear_proj(x)            # (B, embed_dim)

        cls_tokens = self.cls_token.expand(B, -1, -1)  # (B, 1, embed_dim)
        x = torch.cat((cls_tokens, x.unsqueeze(1)), dim=1)  # (B, 2, embed_dim)

        for layer in self.transformer_layers:
            x = layer(x)

        return self.fc_out(x[:, 0])        # Use [CLS] token for classification

# === Test config ===
batch_size = 10
image_size = 32
embed_dim = 128
in_channels = 3
num_heads = 4
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, image_size, image_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes, embed_dim, num_heads]

Let's write a custom C++ CUDA op that does: 1. conv1: `at::conv2d(x, conv1_weight, conv1_bias, patch_size, 0, patch_size)` -> shape [B, C, H/p, W/p] 2. flatten: `x = x.reshape({B, -1})` 3. linear_proj: `x = at::linear(x, linear_proj_weight, linear_proj_bias)` 4. Prepare cls token: `cls = cls_token.expand({B, 1, -1})` 5. Unsqueeze x to [B,1,E] and cat: `x = at::cat({cls, x.unsqueeze(1)}, 1)` 6. For each layer i in 0..num_layers-1: - Self-attention: - qkv = at::linear(x, in_proj_weight[i], in_proj_bias[i]) -> [B, 2, 3E] - split into q,k,v each [B,2,E] - reshape to [B, num_heads, 2, head_dim] - transpose to [B, num_heads, 2, head_dim] for Q K V? Actually for batched matmul need [B*H, 2, D] - Better: view as [B * num_heads, 2, head_dim] for q,k,v. - Compute attn_scores = at::bmm(q, k.transpose(1,2)) / sqrt(head_dim) -> [B*H, 2, 2] - attn_weights = at::softmax(attn_scores, -1) - attn_output = at::bmm(attn_weights, v) -> [B*H, 2, D] - reshape back to [B, 2, E] - out = at::linear(attn_output, out_proj_weight[i], out_proj_bias[i]) - x = x + out - x = at::layer_norm(x, {E}, norm1_weight[i], norm1_bias[i], 1e-5) - FFN: - ff = at::linear(x, linear1_weight[i], linear1_bias[i]) - ff = at::relu(ff) - ff = at::linear(ff, linear2_weight[i], linear2_bias[i]) - x = x + ff - x = at::layer_norm(x, {E}, norm2_weight[i], norm2_bias[i], 1e-5) 7. Extract cls token: `cls_out = x.select(1, 0)` -> [B, E] 8. out = at::linear(cls_out, fc_out_weight, fc_out_bias) Return out. But we need to be careful about `at::layer_norm` parameters. In PyTorch, LayerNorm weight and bias have shape [E]. The eps default is 1e-5. Bias [3E] added. - Split: q = [B, 2, E], k = [B, 2, E], v = [B, 2, E]. - Reshape: q = q.reshape(B, 2, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1,2) -> [B, H, 2, D]. - For computation of attention, they often reshape to [B*H, 2, D] for q,k,v. Then bmm. Our manual approach: q [B, H, 2, D], k [B, H, 2, D]. Compute scores as batched matrix multiplication. We can do: ``` auto scores = at::einsum("bhqd,bhkd->bhqk", {q, k}) / sqrt(head_dim); ``` But `einsum` may be slower or produce different order. Let's use explicit bmm after reshaping. ``` auto q2 = q.reshape({B*H, 2, D}); auto k2 = k.reshape({B*H, 2, D}); auto scores = at::bmm(q2, k2.transpose(1,2)) / sqrt_d; auto attn = at::softmax(scores, -1); auto v2 = v.reshape({B*H, 2, D}); auto out = at::bmm(attn, v2); out = out.reshape({B, H, 2, D}).transpose(1,2).reshape({B, 2, E}); ``` This should match. Activations are tiny. However, weights are read from global memory; we can't avoid that unless we cache them. But we can do the whole forward with each thread processing one batch element. For a given `n`, thread `tid = n` can compute `y[0][n]` and `y[1][n]`. It needs to iterate over `k` and accumulate. Since blockDim.x = 128, we can load W into shared memory in a coalesced way. For a linear with N outputs and K inputs: - Allocate shared memory `sW[chunk_n][K]`. We can process N in chunks of `blockDim.x` (128). Since max N is 512, that's 4 chunks. - For chunk c from 0 to N/128 - 1: - Load W[ c*128 + tid ][k] for k? Actually sW is accessed as `sW[tid*K + k]`; for fixed k, threads 0..127 read addresses spaced by K floats = 512 bytes = 128 4-byte words. Shared memory banks are 4 bytes, 32 banks. So address = (tid*K + k). Since K=128, tid*128 + k. Bank = (tid*128 + k) % 32 = (k) % 32 because 128 mod 32 = 0. So all threads access the same bank for fixed k! That's a 32-way bank conflict. Bad. For a fully-fused per-layer kernel, here's a design: - Each thread block handles one batch element (2 tokens). - The thread block has 128 threads. - Shared memory stores the 2 tokens × 128 dims = 256 floats. - Plus workspace for attention computation. Per thread (say thread d handles dimension d): 1. Load x[0][d] and x[1][d] into shared memory. 2. Layer norm: we need to compute mean and variance of each token. With 128 threads, do warp reductions. 3. Q, K, V: each thread loads the appropriate weights and computes q[0][d], q[1][d], k[0][d], k[1][d], v[0][d], v[1][d]. - Wait, q = x @ W_q, so q[token][d] = sum_j x[token][j] * W_q[j][d]. - This is a matrix multiplication. If we have 128 threads, each thread can compute one output dimension by iterating over input dims. - For 2 tokens, each thread does 2 * reductions over 128 elements. That's 256 multiplies and adds. - Similar for k and v. Total 768 multiply-adds per thread. 4. For attention: we need attention scores per head. With 4 heads and head_dim=32. - We need q[head][token][head_dim] and k[head][token][head_dim]. - Scores[head][i][j] = sum_d q[head][i][d] * k[head][j][d]. - We can have each warp handle one head (32 threads). - Within a warp, compute the 4 scores (2×2) in parallel. But actually each score is a dot product over 32 elements. A warp can do this with shuffle or shared memory. - Actually simpler: have each warp compute one head, and for each score, have all 32 threads participate in the dot product via warp shuffle reduction. 5. Softmax: 2×2 matrix per head. Warp can easily compute this. 6. Attention output: For each head and token, o[head][i][d] = sum_j attn[head][i][j] * v[head][j][d]. - Here, for fixed head and token, we need to compute a weighted sum over the 2 source tokens for each head_dim. - With 32 threads per head (one per head_dim), each thread can do: o[d] = attn[i][0] * v[0][d] + attn[i][1] * v[1][d]. Only 2 multiplies and 1 add! 7. Out projection: o @ W_out^T. Similar to QKV, 128 reductions over 128 elements, for 2 tokens. Then LayerNorm2, MLP, residual. This is definitely doable! Actually, even better: what if I write a kernel that does the entire transformer stack? The kernel would: 1. Load input (B, 2, D) 2. For layer 0 to L-1: a. Load weights for this layer b. Compute layer norm 1 c. Compute self-attention d. Compute MLP e. Store intermediate result 3. Store output The challenge is that weights for 6 layers total about 4.8MB, which needs to be loaded from global memory. But H100 has amazing bandwidth, so that's fine. For the attention on 2 tokens, the algorithm is very simple. Let me write out the exact math for layer norm and attention. LayerNorm(x): mean = sum(x_i) / D var = sum((x_i - mean)^2) / D std = sqrt(var + eps) y_i = (x_i - mean) / std * weight_i + bias_i Self-attention: Q = x @ W_Q^T + b_Q K = x @ W_K^T + b_K V = x @ W_V^T + b_V scores = Q @ K^T / sqrt(D/H) attn = softmax(scores) out = attn @ V out = out @ W_O^T + b_O MLP: h1 = x @ W_FC1^T + b_FC1 h2 = activation(h1) out = h2 @ W_FC2^T + b_FC2 Where activation is either ReLU or GELU. Residual connections everywhere. Now for sequence length 2, we can optimize the attention significantly. scores[i][j] for head h: scores[h][i][j] = sum_d q[h][i][d] * k[h][j][d] / sqrt(head_dim) With 2 tokens, scores is (2, 2). softmax: attn[h][i][j] = exp(scores[h][i][j]) / sum_k exp(scores[h][i][k]) Then: out[h][i][d] = sum_j attn[h][i][j] * v[h][j][d] For 2 tokens, this means: out[h][i][d] = attn[h][i][0] * v[h][0][d] + attn[h][i][1] * v[h][1][d] So each output element is just 2 multiplies and 1 add. Very cheap! This means for a fully-fused kernel, the attention is almost negligible; the linear projections dominate. Let me now plan the kernel implementation. Architecture: - One thread block per batch element - 128 threads per block (4 warps) = per-SM parallelism is good - Each block processes all 6 layers sequentially Shared memory layout: - smem[0..255]: current x (2 tokens * 128 dims) - smem[256..511]: workspace for layer norm / attention - smem[512..1535]: qkv (2 tokens * 3 * 128) - smem[1536..1791]: attention output (2 tokens * 128) - smem[1792..2815]: mlp hidden (2 tokens * 512) Actually, I need to be more careful. Let me think about if this fits in 48KB shared memory: - x: 256 * 4 = 1024 bytes - qkv: 768 * 4 = 3072 bytes - attn_out: 256 * 4 = 1024 bytes - mlp_hidden: 1024 * 4 = 4096 bytes - Total: ~9KB. Very comfortable. For the weights, I'll pass them as separate tensors. But reading 6 layers of weights from global memory inside the kernel means many pointer arguments. CUDA kernels can handle this, but let me see how many arguments we need. Per layer: - norm1_weight, norm1_bias - qkv_weight (3*D, D), qkv_bias (3*D) - out_proj_weight (D, D), out_proj_bias (D) - norm2_weight, norm2_bias - fc1_weight (4*D, D), fc1_bias (4*D) - fc2_weight (D, 4*D), fc2_bias (D) That's 12 tensors per layer × 6 layers = 72 tensor pointers, plus B, D, H, num_layers, etc. CUDA has a limit on kernel arguments, but it's around 4KB, so 72 pointers should be fine. However, managing 72 pointers in the kernel code is a bit annoying. I could pack them into one big tensor. Or just pass them individually. Let me think about an easier way. What if I reorder the weights in Python into a contiguous buffer per layer type? For example: - transformer.fc1_weights: (num_layers, 4*D, D) - transformer.fc2_weights: (num_layers, D, 4*D) - etc. Then in the kernel, I can index layer * stride. This is much cleaner! In Python, I can pre-allocate contiguous buffers: ```python qkv_weights = torch.stack([layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight for layer in model.transformer_layers]) qkv_bias = torch.stack([layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias for layer in model.transformer_layers]) # etc. ``` Then the kernel takes a few big tensors instead of many small ones. Let me think about the PyTorch TransformerEncoderLayer weight organization. For `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=D, nhead=H)`: - `layer.norm1.weight`, `layer.norm1.bias`: shape (D,) - `layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight`: shape (3*D, D) - `layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias`: shape (3*D,) - `layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight`: shape (D, D) - `layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias`: shape (D,) - `layer.norm2.weight`, `layer.norm2.bias`: shape (D,) - `layer.linear1.weight`: shape (dim_feedforward, D) = (4*D, D) - wait, the PyTorch convention might be transposed! Ah, important point: in PyTorch `nn.Linear`, the weight is (out_features, in_features), and the computation is y = x @ W.T + b. So for `nn.Linear(8192, 128)`, W is (128, 8192). For `layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight`, it's a `nn.Linear(D, 3*D)` concatenated, so shape is (3*D, D). For `layer.linear1.weight`, it's `nn.Linear(D, dim_feedforward)`, shape is (dim_feedforward, D). For `layer.linear2.weight`, it's `nn.Linear(dim_feedforward, D)`, shape is (D, dim_feedforward). So when we do matrix multiplies, we need to be careful about transpose. In the kernel, if I compute y = x @ W.T: - If x is (seq, D) and W is (out, D), then y = x @ W.T gives (seq, out). - This is equivalent to y[i][j] = sum_k x[i][k] * W[j][k]. So in the kernel, for each output element (token, out_dim), I sum over in_dim: token_x[in_dim] * W[out_dim][in_dim]. This is a dot product of the token's features with a column of W. Wait, no. If W is stored as (out, in), then W[out_dim] is a row of length in_dim. And x[token] is also length in_dim. The dot product gives y[token][out_dim]. Yes, that's correct. Now, for efficiency in the kernel: - If each thread handles one output dimension, it computes a dot product over in_dim elements. - For in_dim=128 and out_dim=128, each thread does 128 mul-adds for one token. - For 2 tokens: 256 mul-adds. If we have 128 threads, each computing one output dimension for both tokens, the parallelism is good. For the MLP fc1: in_dim=128, out_dim=512. If each thread handles one output dimension, we'd need 512 threads. But we only have 128 threads. So each thread would need to handle 512/128 = 4 output dimensions. Alternatively, we could have 256 or 512 threads per block. Let me think about what's best. With 128 threads: - For (128->128) projections: 1 output dim per thread, both tokens - For (128->512) projection: 4 output dims per thread, both tokens - For (512->128) projection: 1 output dim per thread (since 512 input, 128 output), but each thread sums over 512 elements instead of 128. That's fine. Wait, actually for 512->128, each output dim is a dot product over 512 elements. With 128 threads, each handles one output dim for both tokens. 512 mul-adds per token = 1024 mul-adds per thread. Still very manageable. For fc1 (128->512), we could either: - Have 128 threads each compute 4 output dims: 128 * 4 = 512 - Or use 512 threads I think keeping 128 threads and having each thread handle multiple output dimensions is fine and keeps block size uniform. Let me design the kernel with 128 threads per block, one block per batch element. Algorithm per block: For each layer l from 0 to L-1: 1. Load x[0..1][0..D-1] into shared memory 2. LayerNorm1 on both tokens - Compute mean and variance per token - Normalize and apply scale/bias - Store normalized x back to smem 3. Self-attention: a. Compute QKV: for each token and each of 3*D outputs - Each thread handles 3 output dims? Wait, 3*D = 384 outputs. - With 128 threads, each thread handles 3 output dims. - Actually, to simplify, let's have each thread compute one output dim. But QKV has 384 outputs, so we'd need 384 threads. - Alternative: loop. Each thread computes Q, then K, then V for its assigned dims. b. Reorganize qkv into head-major format in shared memory c. Compute attention scores and softmax per head d. Compute attention output e. Out projection 4. Residual add: x = x + attn_out 5. LayerNorm2 6. MLP: a. FC1 (128 -> 512) b. Activation c. FC2 (512 -> 128) 7. Residual add: x = x + mlp_out Wait, for step 3a, QKV computation: - QKV weight is (3*D, D). We need to compute for each token: token_feats @ W_qkv.T. - Output shape: (2, 3*D). - With 128 threads, each thread can handle 3 consecutive output dims (since 384 / 128 = 3). - For each of the 3 output dims, the thread computes the dot product over 128 input dims for both tokens. That works: thread t handles output dims {t, t+128, t+256} for both tokens. Let me verify: 128 threads * 3 outputs = 384 = 3*D. Yes. Similarly, for out projection: weight is (D, D). 128 threads * 1 output = 128 = D. Perfect. For FC1: weight is (4*D, D) = (512, 128). 128 threads * 4 outputs = 512. Each thread handles 4 output dims. For FC2: weight is (D, 4*D) = (128, 512). 128 threads * 1 output = 128. Each thread handles 1 output dim, dot product over 512 elements. This all works out nicely with 128 threads! Now, for attention on 2 tokens: After computing QKV, we have for each token: q (128), k (128), v (128). We need to split into heads: num_heads=4, head_dim=32. Rewriting in head-major: For head h in 0..3: For token t in 0..1: q_h[t][d] = q[t][h*head_dim + d] k_h[t][d] = k[t][h*head_dim + d] v_h[t][d] = v[t][h*head_dim + d] Then for each head h: For query token i in 0..1: For key token j in 0..1: score[h][i][j] = dot(q_h[i], k_h[j]) / sqrt(head_dim) Softmax over j: attn[h][i][j] = exp(score[h][i][j]) / sum_k exp(score[h][i][k]) For output token i in 0..1: For dim d in 0..head_dim-1: o_h[i][d] = attn[h][i][0] * v_h[0][d] + attn[h][i][1] * v_h[1][d] Then flatten back: out[t][h*head_dim + d] = o_h[t][d] How to parallelize this with 128 threads? - We could have each warp (32 threads) handle one head. - Within a warp, thread d handles dimension d (0..31). - For the scores: we need to compute 4 dot products (2 query × 2 key). - To compute score[i][j], all 32 threads need to compute dot(q[i], k[j]). - We can do this with warp shuffle, or by having all threads load q and k, multiply, and reduce. - For the attention output: thread d computes o[i][d] = attn[i][0] * v[0][d] + attn[i][1] * v[1][d]. This is clean! Warp-level dot product using shuffle: ```cuda float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < head_dim; d += 32) { dot += q[d] * k[d]; } // Reduce within warp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } ``` But wait, if each thread in the warp handles one unique d, and head_dim=32, then thread 0 handles d=0, thread 1 handles d=1, ..., thread 31 handles d=31. No loop needed! Just one load and multiply per thread. Then reduce using shuffle. After the reduction, all 32 threads have the dot product. Then compute softmax. The softmax for a 2-token sequence: Given a and b: max_val = max(a, b) sum_exp = exp(a - max_val) + exp(b - max_val) soft_a = exp(a - max_val) / sum_exp soft_b = exp(b - max_val) / sum_exp This is trivial. Then compute the weighted v values. Now, where do we store q, k, v for the attention computation? We can store QKV in shared memory. Since QKV is (2, 3, 128) = 768 floats = 3KB. Actually, do we even need shared memory for QKV? We could keep them in registers if we want: Each thread computes 3 QKV values (for its 3 assigned dims). But for attention, we need all q, k, v values within a head. So we do need to share them across threads, which means shared memory. So QKV goes in shared memory. Similarly, the attention output goes in shared memory. For the layer norm and linear projections, shared memory for x is also needed. OK, let me now think about writing the CUDA code. I'll need to figure out the activation function. Let me assume GELU for now, but I might need to check. Actually, GELU is more complex than ReLU. ReLU is just max(0, x). GELU is: GELU(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/π) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) or using the erf approximation. For correctness with the reference, I need to know which one PyTorch uses. In PyTorch, `nn.GELU()` uses the tanh approximation by default. Fine. Now for the transformer kernel itself, let me draft the CUDA code. Actually wait - I need to be careful about the `nn.MultiheadAttention` implementation in PyTorch. The `in_proj_weight` for MultiheadAttention combines W_q, W_k, W_v into one tensor of shape (3*D, D). The `out_proj` has weight of shape (D, D). Also, for `bias` in MultiheadAttention, `in_proj_bias` is shape (3*D,), and `out_proj.bias` is shape (D,). Another thing: are the Q, K, V weights stored as (D, D) each, concatenated along dim 0? Yes, `in_proj_weight` is (3*D, D), where the first D rows are W_q, next D are W_k, last D are W_v. Same for bias. Now, in the attention computation, for `batch_first=True` and multi-head attention: - Input: (B, N, D) - Q, K, V are computed as input @ W_q.T, etc. - Q, K, V are reshaped to (B, N, H, D/H) and transposed to (B, H, N, D/H) - Attention scores: Q @ K.T / sqrt(D/H) -> (B, H, N, N) - Softmax - Output = attn @ V -> (B, H, N, D/H) - Transpose and reshape to (B, N, D) - Output projection For N=2, H=4, D/H=32. In the kernel, I need to match this exactly. Alright, let me write the kernel. I'll define it as: ```cuda __global__ void fused_transformer_kernel( const float* input, // (B, 2, D) float* output, // (B, 2, D) // Layer 0 weights const float* norm1_weights, // (L, D) const float* norm1_biases, // (L, D) const float* qkv_weights, // (L, 3*D, D) const float* qkv_biases, // (L, 3*D) const float* out_proj_weights, // (L, D, D) const float* out_proj_biases, // (L, D) const float* norm2_weights, // (L, D) const float* norm2_biases, // (L, D) const float* fc1_weights, // (L, 4*D, D) const float* fc1_biases, // (L, 4*D) const float* fc2_weights, // (L, D, 4*D) const float* fc2_biases, // (L, D) int B, int L, int D, int H ) ``` Where `input` and `output` can point to the same buffer (in-place). Actually, if input and output are the same, I need to be careful. For safety, I'll use separate buffers and copy. Hmm, with 10 batch elements and 2 tokens × 128 dims = 2560 floats, copying is trivial. But actually, if the kernel is in-place, each layer reads from and writes to shared memory, flushing to global memory only at layer boundaries. This is fine. Let me make the kernel in-place: it reads x from global memory at the start, processes all layers, and writes back. Actually, no - for in-place with one block per batch element, each block loads its 2 tokens into shared memory, processes all layers using shared memory as workspace, and writes back at the end. This is perfect! Wait, but if all blocks independently load and process, how do they know which batch element to process? Each block uses `blockIdx.x`. With B blocks (one per batch element), block b processes batch element b. OK so here's the kernel structure: ```cuda extern "C" __global__ void fused_transformer_forward( const float* x, // input/output, size B * 2 * D // weights... int B, int L, int D, int H, int Dh) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // smem layout: // buf1: 2 * D (current activation) // buf2: 2 * D (workspace for norm/attn out) // qkv: 2 * 3 * D // mlp: 2 * 4 * D float* buf1 = smem; float* buf2 = smem + 2 * D; float* qkv_smem = smem + 4 * D; float* mlp_smem = smem + 4 * D + 6 * D; // 2 * 3 * D = 6*D // Load x into buf1 float* x_ptr = x + b * 2 * D; for (int i = tid; i < 2 * D; i += blockDim.x) { buf1[i] = x_ptr[i]; } __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < L; l++) { // --- Self-attention block --- // Load weights for this layer const float* nw = norm1_weights + l * D; const float* nb = norm1_biases + l * D; const float* qw = qkv_weights + l * 3 * D * D; const float* qb = qkv_biases + l * 3 * D; const float* ow = out_proj_weights + l * D * D; const float* ob = out_proj_biases + l * D; // Layer norm on buf1 -> buf2 for (int token = 0; token < 2; token++) { // Compute mean float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { sum += buf1[token * D + d]; } // warp reduce for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } __shared__ float mean; if (tid == 0) mean = sum / D; __syncthreads(); // Compute variance float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float diff = buf1[token * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, var_sum, offset); } __shared__ float inv_std; if (tid == 0) inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); __syncthreads(); // Normalize and apply weights for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = (buf1[token * D + d] - mean) * inv_std; buf2[token * D + d] = val * nw[d] + nb[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // QKV projection: buf2 -> qkv_smem // For each of 3*D outputs, each thread handles 3 outputs for (int out_idx = tid; out_idx < 3 * D; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float sum0 = 0.0f, sum1 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float w = qw[out_idx * D + d]; // Wait, shape is (3*D, D) sum0 += buf2[0 * D + d] * w; sum1 += buf2[1 * D + d] * w; } qkv_smem[0 * 3 * D + out_idx] = sum0 + qb[out_idx]; qkv_smem[1 * 3 * D + out_idx] = sum1 + qb[out_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention and out projection // We'll use warp-level parallelism for heads int head = tid / 32; // 0 to 3 int lane = tid % 32; if (tid < 4 * 32) { // only first 128 threads participate, which is all of them float q[2], k[2], v[2]; // For each token for (int token = 0; token < 2; token++) { q[token] = qkv_smem[token * 3 * D + head * Dh + lane]; // wait, this only works for lane < Dh=32. But we have 32 lanes per head, and Dh=32. Yes, lane goes 0..31. k[token] = qkv_smem[token * 3 * D + D + head * Dh + lane]; v[token] = qkv_smem[token * 3 * D + 2 * D + head * Dh + lane]; } // Compute scores for both query tokens float score00 = 0.0f, score01 = 0.0f, score10 = 0.0f, score11 = 0.0f; // Actually, can compute with warp shuffle // But simpler: each lane computes its partial product, then reduce // Score[q_i][k_j] = sum_d q_i[d] * k_j[d] // For query 0: float q0k0 = q[0] * k[0]; float q0k1 = q[0] * k[1]; // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { q0k0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q0k0, offset); q0k1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q0k1, offset); } // For query 1: float q1k0 = q[1] * k[0]; float q1k1 = q[1] * k[1]; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { q1k0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q1k0, offset); q1k1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q1k1, offset); } // Only lane 0 has the correct sums, broadcast to all score00 = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, q0k0, 0); score01 = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, q0k1, 0); score10 = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, q1k0, 0); score11 = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, q1k1, 0); float scale = rsqrtf((float)Dh); score00 *= scale; score01 *= scale; score10 *= scale; score11 *= scale; // Softmax for query 0 float max0 = fmaxf(score00, score01); float exp00 = expf(score00 - max0); float exp01 = expf(score01 - max0); float sum0 = exp00 + exp01; float attn00 = exp00 / sum0; float attn01 = exp01 / sum0; // Softmax for query 1 float max1 = fmaxf(score10, score11); float exp10 = expf(score10 - max1); float exp11 = expf(score11 - max1); float sum1 = exp10 + exp11; float attn10 = exp10 / sum1; float attn11 = exp11 / sum1; // Compute attention output float out0 = attn00 * v[0] + attn01 * v[1]; float out1 = attn10 * v[0] + attn11 * v[1]; // Store back to qkv_smem or buf2 // Need to make sure we're not overwriting qkv before all warps are done // But each warp operates on independent head data buf2[0 * D + head * Dh + lane] = out0; buf2[1 * D + head * Dh + lane] = out1; } __syncthreads(); // Out projection: buf2 -> qkv_smem (used as temp) for (int out_d = tid; out_d < D; out_d += blockDim.x) { float sum0 = 0.0f, sum1 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float w = ow[out_d * D + d]; sum0 += buf2[0 * D + d] * w; sum1 += buf2[1 * D + d] * w; } qkv_smem[0 * D + out_d] = sum0 + ob[out_d]; qkv_smem[1 * D + out_d] = sum1 + ob[out_d]; } __syncthreads(); // Residual add: buf1 + qkv_smem -> buf2 (or buf1) for (int i = tid; i < 2 * D; i += blockDim.x) { buf1[i] = buf1[i] + qkv_smem[i]; } __syncthreads(); // --- MLP block --- const float* n2w = norm2_weights + l * D; const float* n2b = norm2_biases + l * D; const float* f1w = fc1_weights + l * 4 * D * D; const float* f1b = fc1_biases + l * 4 * D; const float* f2w = fc2_weights + l * D * 4 * D; const float* f2b = fc2_biases + l * D; // Layer norm 2 for (int token = 0; token < 2; token++) { // same as layer norm 1 but on buf1 // ... } // FC1: buf2 -> mlp_smem for (int out_d = tid; out_d < 4 * D; out_d += blockDim.x) { float sum0 = 0.0f, sum1 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float w = f1w[out_d * D + d]; sum0 += buf2[0 * D + d] * w; sum1 += buf2[1 * D + d] * w; } float val0 = sum0 + f1b[out_d]; float val1 = sum1 + f1b[out_d]; // Activation #ifdef USE_RELU val0 = fmaxf(val0, 0.0f); val1 = fmaxf(val1, 0.0f); #else // GELU val0 = val0 * 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (val0 + 0.044715f * val0 * val0 * val0))); val1 = val1 * 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (val1 + 0.044715f * val1 * val1 * val1))); #endif mlp_smem[0 * 4 * D + out_d] = val0; mlp_smem[1 * 4 * D + out_d] = val1; } __syncthreads(); // FC2: mlp_smem -> qkv_smem (as temp) for (int out_d = tid; out_d < D; out_d += blockDim.x) { float sum0 = 0.0f, sum1 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < 4 * D; d++) { float w = f2w[out_d * 4 * D + d]; sum0 += mlp_smem[0 * 4 * D + d] * w; sum1 += mlp_smem[1 * 4 * D + d] * w; } qkv_smem[0 * D + out_d] = sum0 + f2b[out_d]; qkv_smem[1 * D + out_d] = sum1 + f2b[out_d]; } __syncthreads(); // Residual add for (int i = tid; i < 2 * D; i += blockDim.x) { buf1[i] = buf1[i] + qkv_smem[i]; } __syncthreads(); } // Write back to global memory for (int i = tid; i < 2 * D; i += blockDim.x) { x_ptr[i] = buf1[i]; } } ``` Hmm, there are a lot of details to get right. Let me think about potential issues: 1. Shared memory layout and bank conflicts. With 2*D = 256 floats, and 128 threads each accessing adjacent elements, if each thread accesses one float, there will be 4 bytes per thread, and with bank width 4 bytes on H100, each warp accesses 128 consecutive floats = 128 consecutive banks. If 32 threads access 32 consecutive floats, that's 32 banks, no conflicts. But what about the second warp (threads 32-63)? They access floats 32-63. Also fine. So row-major layout is good. 2. Warp-level synchronization. The `__syncthreads()` calls ensure all threads in the block are synchronized, but for warp-level operations like shuffle, lanes 0-31 operate independently from 32-63, etc. Since each head is processed by one warp, and heads are independent, this is fine. 3. The QKV weight indexing. For `qw + out_idx * D + d`, this assumes row-major where row `out_idx` has length `D`. Weight tensor shape is (3*D, D), which in PyTorch is row-major on GPU, so `qw[out_idx * D + d]` is correct. 4. For FC1, weight shape is (4*D, D), so `f1w[out_d * D + d]` is correct. 5. For FC2, weight shape is (D, 4*D), so `f2w[out_d * 4*D + d]` is correct. 6. For out_proj, weight shape is (D, D), so `ow[out_d * D + d]` is correct. 7. Layer norm mean/variance reduction: I use warp shuffle only, but with 128 threads and D=128, this only uses the first 4 warps. Actually, all 128 threads participate. For mean, each thread sums D/blockDim.x = 128/128 = 1 element. So each thread loads exactly one element. The warp shuffle reduces within each warp of 32 threads, giving a partial sum per warp. But I only reduce within the warp and then broadcast. I need to also reduce across warps! Wait, this is a bug in my pseudocode. Let me fix: For mean/variance: ```cuda float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { sum += buf1[token * D + d]; } // Reduce within warp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } // Reduce across warps __shared__ float warp_sums[4]; // 4 warps if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = sum; __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { float total = warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]; mean = total / D; } __syncthreads(); ``` Same for variance. Actually, since D=128 and blockDim.x=128, each thread handles exactly 1 element. So the loop `for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x)` runs exactly once per thread. The reduction is still needed. Let me refine the layer norm code: ```cuda // Compute mean float local = buf1[token * D + tid]; // warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local, offset); } __shared__ float warp_sums[4]; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = local; __syncthreads(); __shared__ float mean_val; if (tid == 0) { mean_val = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D; } __syncthreads(); float mean = mean_val; // Compute variance local = (buf1[token * D + tid] - mean) * (buf1[token * D + tid] - mean); for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = local; __syncthreads(); __shared__ float inv_std_val; if (tid == 0) { inv_std_val = rsqrtf((warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D + 1e-5f); } __syncthreads(); float inv_std = inv_std_val; // Normalize buf2[token * D + tid] = (buf1[token * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std * nw[tid] + nb[tid]; ``` Wait, there's an issue here. When D=128 and blockDim.x=128, `tid` ranges from 0 to 127. But for token=0, I access `buf1[0 * D + tid]`, and for token=1, `buf1[1 * D + tid]`. With 32 banks (common), a warp accesses all banks exactly once, no conflicts. Good. For `qkv_smem[token * 3*D + out_idx]`, when threads compute QKV, `out_idx` varies by thread. I can inline it or write a device function. ```cuda __device__ void layer_norm(const float* in, float* out, const float* w, const float* b, int D, int tid) { __shared__ float warp_sums[4]; __shared__ float shared_mean; __shared__ float shared_inv_std; int lane = tid & 31; int wid = tid >> 5; for (int token = 0; token < 2; token++) { float local = in[token * D + tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[wid] = local; __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { shared_mean = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D; } __syncthreads(); float mean = shared_mean; local = (in[token * D + tid] - mean) * (in[token * D + tid] - mean); #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[wid] = local; __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { shared_inv_std = rsqrtf((warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D + 1e-5f); } __syncthreads(); float inv_std = shared_inv_std; out[token * D + tid] = (in[token * D + tid] - mean) * inv_std * w[tid] + b[tid]; __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, I call `__syncthreads()` at the end of each token iteration. This ensures all threads have finished writing to `out` before `out` might be read as `in` in a future call. Good. But note: in the layer_norm function, `in` and `out` might both point to shared memory. I pass `float*` pointers. Actually, wait. Let me rewrite the layer norm to be general: ```cuda __device__ void layer_norm(const float* in, float* out, const float* w, const float* b, int D, int tid, int num_threads) { __shared__ float warp_sums[4]; __shared__ float shared_mean; __shared__ float shared_inv_std; int lane = tid & 31; int wid = tid >> 5; for (int token = 0; token < 2; token++) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { sum += in[token * D + d]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[wid] = sum; __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { shared_mean = (warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D; } __syncthreads(); float mean = shared_mean; float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { float diff = in[token * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, var_sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sums[wid] = var_sum; __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { shared_inv_std = rsqrtf((warp_sums[0] + warp_sums[1] + warp_sums[2] + warp_sums[3]) / D + 1e-5f); } __syncthreads(); float inv_std = shared_inv_std; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { out[token * D + d] = (in[token * D + d] - mean) * inv_std * w[d] + b[d]; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` This is general for any D, as long as blockDim.x is a multiple of 32 (for warp operations) and >= 32. For test config, D=128, num_threads=128. Each loop runs once. OK, now for QKV. Same pattern: ```cuda for (int out_base = 0; out_base < (3 * D + num_threads - 1) / num_threads; out_base++) { int out_idx = tid + out_base * num_threads; if (out_idx < 3 * D) { float sum0 = 0.0f, sum1 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float w = qw[out_idx * D + d]; sum0 += act[0 * D + d] * w; sum1 += act[1 * D + d] * w; } qkv[0 * 3 * D + out_idx] = sum0 + qb[out_idx]; qkv[1 * 3 * D + out_idx] = sum1 + qb[out_idx]; } } __syncthreads(); ``` For D=128 and num_threads=128: 3*D=384, so `out_base` runs 0, 1, 2 (since 384/128 = 3). Each thread handles 3 output dims (tid, tid+128, tid+256). The inner loop over d runs 128 times. This is correct. For attention: I need to make sure all threads are used. With num_threads=128 and H=4, Dh=32. - tid 0..31: head 0, lane 0..31 - tid 32..63: head 1, lane 0..31 - tid 64..95: head 2, lane 0..31 - tid 96..127: head 3, lane 0..31 Each thread loads q, k, v for both tokens from qkv_smem: ```cuda int head = tid >> 5; int lane = tid & 31; int Dh = D / num_heads; float q0 = qkv[0 * 3 * D + head * Dh + lane]; float k0 = qkv[0 * 3 * D + D + head * Dh + lane]; float v0 = qkv[0 * 3 * D + 2 * D + head * Dh + lane]; float q1 = qkv[1 * 3 * D + head * Dh + lane]; float k1 = qkv[1 * 3 * D + D + head * Dh + lane]; float v1 = qkv[1 * 3 * D + 2 * D + head * Dh + lane]; ``` Wait, is the indexing correct? - For token 0, Q starts at `0 * 3 * D + 0` - K starts at `0 * 3 * D + D` - V starts at `0 * 3 * D + 2 * D` For head h and lane d: - Q: `head * Dh + lane` - K: `D + head * Dh + lane` - V: `2 * D + head * Dh + lane` Yes, this is correct. head * Dh + lane];\n\n float q0k0 = q0 * k0;\n float q0k1 = q0 * k1;\n float q1k0 = q1 * k0;\n float q1k1 = q1 * k1;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n q0k0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q0k0, offset);\n q0k1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q0k1, offset);\n q1k0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q1k0, offset);\n q1k1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, q1k1, scale; q0k1 *= scale;\n q1k0 *= scale; q1k1 *= scale;\n\n float max0 = fmaxf(q0k0, q0k1);\n float exp00 = expf(q0k0 - max0);\n float exp01 = expf(q0k1 - max0);\n float sum0 = exp00 + exp01;\n float a00 = exp00 / sum0;\n float a01 = exp01 / sum0;\n\n float max1 = fmaxf(q1k0, q1k1);\n float exp10 = expf(q1k0 - max1);\n float exp11 = expf(q1k1 - max1);\n float sum1 = exp10 + exp11;\n float a10 = The problem is that `cpp_sources` gets compiled with `g++`, which doesn't understand CUDA syntax. So we need to put the host wrapper in `cuda_sources` so it's compiled by nvcc. Actually, for C++ linking, the function just needs to have the same signature. Since both files are compiled in the same shared library, the linker should resolve it.
